<p><b>Karl Emil Maximilian</b> "<b>Max</b>" <b>Weber</b> (<small>German:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_German" title="Help:IPA for German">[ˈmaks ˈveːbɐ]</a></span>; 21 April 1864 – 14 June 1920) was a <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociologist</a>, philosopher, jurist, and <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economist</a> whose ideas profoundly influenced <a href="/wiki/Social_theory" title="Social theory">social theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_research" title="Social research">social research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Weber is often cited, with <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, as among the three founders of sociology.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WeberGerth1991_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WeberGerth1991-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Weber was a key proponent of <a href="/wiki/Antipositivism" title="Antipositivism">methodological antipositivism</a>, arguing for the study of <a href="/wiki/Social_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Social action">social action</a> through <a href="/wiki/Verstehen" title="Verstehen">interpretive</a> (rather than purely <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricist</a>) means, based on understanding the purpose and meaning that individuals attach to their own actions. Unlike Durkheim, he did not believe in monocausality and rather proposed that for any outcome there can be multiple causes.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber's main intellectual concern was understanding the processes of <a href="/wiki/Rationalisation_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rationalisation (sociology)">rationalisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secularisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Secularisation">secularisation</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">disenchantment</a>" that he associated with the rise of capitalism and <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Habermas.2C_J.C3.BCrgen_1990_p2_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Habermas.2C_J.C3.BCrgen_1990_p2-11">[11]</a></sup> and which he saw as the result of a new way of thinking about the world.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Weber is best known for his thesis combining <a href="/wiki/Economic_sociology" title="Economic sociology">economic sociology</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociology of religion</a>, elaborated in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i>, in which he proposed that <a href="/wiki/Ascetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> was one of the major "elective affinities" associated with the rise in the Western world of <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market</a>-driven capitalism and the <a href="/wiki/Rational-legal" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational-legal">rational-legal</a> <a href="/wiki/Nation-state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nation-state">nation-state</a>. He argued that it was in the basic tenets of Protestantism to boost capitalism. Thus, it can be said that the spirit of capitalism is inherent to Protestant religious values.</p>
<p>Against Marx's <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, Weber emphasised the importance of cultural influences embedded in religion as a means for understanding the genesis of capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The <i>Protestant Ethic</i> formed the earliest part in Weber's broader investigations into world religion; he went on to examine the <a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_China:_Confucianism_and_Taoism" title="The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism">religions of China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_India:_The_Sociology_of_Hinduism_and_Buddhism" title="The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism">religions of India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Judaism_(book)" title="Ancient Judaism (book)">ancient Judaism</a>, with particular regard to their differing economic consequences and conditions of <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_anone"><a href="#endnote_anone">[a]</a></sup> In another major work, "<a href="/wiki/Politics_as_a_Vocation" title="Politics as a Vocation">Politics as a Vocation</a>", Weber defined the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> as an entity that successfully claims a "<a href="/wiki/Monopoly_on_violence" title="Monopoly on violence">monopoly of the legitimate use of physical force</a> within a given territory". He was also the first to categorise social authority into distinct forms, which he labelled as charismatic, traditional, and rational-legal. His analysis of <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a> emphasised that modern state institutions are increasingly based on <a href="/wiki/Rational-legal_authority" title="Rational-legal authority">rational-legal authority</a>.</p>
<p>Weber also made a variety of other contributions in <a href="/wiki/Economic_history" title="Economic history">economic history</a>, as well as economic theory and methodology. Weber's analysis of modernity and rationalisation significantly influenced the <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">critical theory</a> associated with the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_School" title="Frankfurt School">Frankfurt School</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, Max Weber was among the founders of the liberal <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Party" title="German Democratic Party">German Democratic Party</a>. He also ran unsuccessfully for a seat in parliament and served as advisor to the committee that drafted the ill-fated democratic <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Constitution" title="Weimar Constitution">Weimar Constitution</a> of 1919. After contracting <a href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">Spanish flu</a>, he died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> in 1920, aged 56.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family_background">Early life and family background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life and family background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Karl Emil Maximilian Weber was born in 1864, in <a href="/wiki/Erfurt" title="Erfurt">Erfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Saxony" title="Province of Saxony">Province of Saxony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> He was the oldest of the seven children of <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber_Sr." title="Max Weber Sr.">Max Weber Sr.</a>, a wealthy and prominent civil servant and member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberal_Party_(Germany)" title="National Liberal Party (Germany)">National Liberal Party</a>, and his wife Helene (Fallenstein), who partly descended from French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> immigrants and held strong moral absolutist ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber Sr.'s involvement in public life immersed his home in both politics and academia, as his salon welcomed many prominent scholars and public figures.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> The young Weber and his brother <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Weber" title="Alfred Weber">Alfred</a>, who also became a sociologist and economist, thrived in this intellectual atmosphere. Weber's 1876 Christmas presents to his parents, when he was thirteen years old, were two historical essays entitled "About the course of German history, with special reference to the positions of the Emperor and the Pope", and "About the Roman Imperial period from Constantine to the migration of nations".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In class, bored and unimpressed with the teachers&#160;– who in turn resented what they perceived as a disrespectful attitude&#160;– he secretly read all forty volumes of <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-K.C3.A4sler1988_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K.C3.A4sler1988-17">[17]</a></sup> and it has been recently argued that this was an important influence on his thought and methodology.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Before entering the university, he would read many other classical works.<sup id="cite_ref-K.C3.A4sler1988_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K.C3.A4sler1988-17">[17]</a></sup> Over time, Weber would also be significantly affected by the marital tension between his father, "a man who enjoyed earthly pleasures", and his mother, a devout <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> "who sought to lead an <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetic</a> life".<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-32_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-32-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LK_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LK-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg/170px-Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg/255px-Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg/340px-Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg 2x" data-file-width="394" data-file-height="534" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Weber_and_brothers_1879.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Max Weber and his brothers, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Weber" title="Alfred Weber">Alfred</a> and Karl, in 1879</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1882 Weber enrolled in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Heidelberg" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Heidelberg">University of Heidelberg</a> as a law student.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix1-21">[21]</a></sup> After a year of military service, he transferred to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup> After his first few years as a student, during which he spent much time "drinking beer and <a href="/wiki/Academic_fencing" title="Academic fencing">fencing</a>", Weber would increasingly take his mother's side in family arguments and grew estranged from his father.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-32_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-32-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LK_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LK-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-146_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-146-22">[22]</a></sup> Simultaneously with his studies, he worked as a junior lawyer.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup> In 1886 Weber passed the examination for <i><a href="/wiki/Referendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Referendar">Referendar</a></i>, comparable to the <a href="/wiki/Bar_association" title="Bar association">bar association</a> examination in the British and American legal systems. Throughout the late 1880s, Weber continued his study of law and history.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup> He earned his law doctorate in 1889 by writing a <a href="/wiki/Thesis" title="Thesis">dissertation</a> on legal history titled <i>Development of the Principle of Joint Liability and the Separate Fund in the Public Trading Company out of Household and Trade Communities in Italian Cities</i>. This work was used as part of a longer work <i>On the History of Trading Companies in the Middle Ages, based on South-European Sources</i>, published in the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Two years later, Weber completed his <a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">Habilitationsschrift</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_Agrarian_History_and_its_Significance_for_Public_and_Private_Law_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Agrarian History and its Significance for Public and Private Law (book)">Roman Agrarian History and its Significance for Public and Private Law</a></i>, working with <a href="/wiki/August_Meitzen" title="August Meitzen">August Meitzen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-diss_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diss-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bendix2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix2-25">[25]</a></sup> Having thus become a <i><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">Privatdozent</a></i>, Weber joined the University of Berlin's faculty, lecturing and consulting for the government.<sup id="cite_ref-Lachmann1970_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lachmann1970-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_work">Early work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the years between the completion of his dissertation and habilitation, Weber took an interest in contemporary <a href="/wiki/Social_policy" title="Social policy">social policy</a>. In 1888 he joined the <i><a href="/wiki/Verein_f%C3%BCr_Socialpolitik" title="Verein für Socialpolitik">Verein für Socialpolitik</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-WaSI_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaSI-27">[27]</a></sup> a new professional association of German economists affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Historical_school_of_economics" title="Historical school of economics">historical school</a>, who saw the role of economics primarily as finding solutions to the social problems of the age and who pioneered large scale statistical studies of economic issues. He also involved himself in politics, joining the left-leaning <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Social_Congress" title="Evangelical Social Congress">Evangelical Social Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Max_Weber_and_German_Politics.2C_1890.E2.80.931920_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Max_Weber_and_German_Politics.2C_1890.E2.80.931920-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1890 the <i>Verein</i> established a research program to examine "the Polish question" or <i><a href="/wiki/Ostflucht" title="Ostflucht">Ostflucht</a></i>: the influx of Polish farm workers into <a href="/wiki/Former_eastern_territories_of_Germany" title="Former eastern territories of Germany">eastern Germany</a> as local labourers migrated to Germany's rapidly <a href="/wiki/Industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialization">industrialising</a> cities.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber was put in charge of the study and wrote a large part of the final report,<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WaSI_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaSI-27">[27]</a></sup> which generated considerable attention and controversy and marked the beginning of Weber's renown as a social scientist.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> From 1893 to 1899 Weber was a member of the <i><a href="/wiki/Alldeutscher_Verband" class="mw-redirect" title="Alldeutscher Verband">Alldeutscher Verband</a></i> (Pan-German League), an organization that campaigned against the influx of the Polish workers; the degree of Weber's support for the <a href="/wiki/Germanisation_of_Poles_during_the_Partitions" title="Germanisation of Poles during the Partitions">Germanisation of Poles</a> and similar nationalist policies is still debated by modern scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-MommsenSteinberg1990_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MommsenSteinberg1990-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hobsbawm1987_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hobsbawm1987-30">[30]</a></sup> In some of his work, in particular his provocative lecture on "The Nation State and Economic Policy" delivered in 1895, Weber criticises the immigration of Poles and blames the <a href="/wiki/Junker_(Prussia)" title="Junker (Prussia)">Junker</a> class for perpetuating Slavic immigration to serve their selfish interests.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg/170px-Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg/255px-Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg/340px-Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="581" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_and_Marianne_Weber_1894.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Max Weber and his wife Marianne in 1894</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Also in 1893 he married his distant cousin <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Weber" title="Marianne Weber">Marianne Schnitger</a>, later a feminist activist and author in her own right,<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marianne_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marianne-32">[32]</a></sup> who was instrumental in collecting and publishing Weber's journal articles as books after his death, while her biography of him is an important source for understanding Weber's life.<sup id="cite_ref-LehmannRoth1995_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LehmannRoth1995-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HonigsheimSica2003_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HonigsheimSica2003-34">[34]</a></sup> They would have no children and it is usually acknowledged that their marriage was never consummated.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-146_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-146-22">[22]</a></sup> The marriage granted long-awaited financial independence to Weber, allowing him to finally leave his parents' household.<sup id="cite_ref-LK_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LK-20">[20]</a></sup> The couple moved to <a href="/wiki/Freiburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Freiburg">Freiburg</a> in 1894, where Weber was appointed professor of economics at the <a href="/wiki/Albert-Ludwigs-Universit%C3%A4t" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert-Ludwigs-Universität">university</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix2-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lachmann1970_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lachmann1970-26">[26]</a></sup> before accepting the same position at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Heidelberg" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Heidelberg">University of Heidelberg</a> in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix2_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix2-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lachmann1970_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lachmann1970-26">[26]</a></sup> There Weber became a central figure in the so-called "Weber Circle", composed of other intellectuals such as his wife Marianne, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Jellinek" title="Georg Jellinek">Georg Jellinek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Troeltsch" title="Ernst Troeltsch">Ernst Troeltsch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bloch" title="Marc Bloch">Marc Bloch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Michels" title="Robert Michels">Robert Michels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Luk%C3%A1cs" title="György Lukács">György Lukács</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber also remained active in the <i>Verein</i> and the Evangelical Social Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> His research in that period was focused on economics and legal history.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-166_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-166-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1897 Max Weber Sr. died two months after a severe quarrel with his son that was never resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EESoc-7_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EESoc-7-36">[36]</a></sup> After this, Weber became increasingly prone to depression, nervousness and <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, making it difficult for him to fulfill his duties as a professor.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bendix2_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix2-25">[25]</a></sup> His condition forced him to reduce his teaching and eventually leave his course unfinished in the autumn of 1899. After spending months in a sanatorium during the summer and autumn of 1900, Weber and his wife travelled to Italy at the end of the year and did not return to Heidelberg until April 1902. He would again withdraw from teaching in 1903 and not return to it till 1919. Weber's ordeal with mental illness was carefully described in a personal chronology that was destroyed by his wife. This chronicle was supposedly destroyed because Marianne Weber feared that Max Weber's work would be discredited by the Nazis if his experience with mental illness were widely known.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newyorker_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_work">Later work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Later work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After Weber's immense productivity in the early 1890s, he did not publish any papers between early 1898 and late 1902, finally resigning his professorship in late 1903. Freed from those obligations, in that year he accepted a position as associate editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Archiv_f%C3%BCr_Sozialwissenschaft_und_Sozialpolitik" title="Archiv für Sozialwissenschaft und Sozialpolitik">Archives for Social Science and Social Welfare</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix3_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix3-38">[38]</a></sup> where he worked with his colleagues <a href="/w/index.php?title=Edgar_Jaff%C3%A9&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Edgar Jaffé (page does not exist)">Edgar Jaffé</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_Jaff%C3%A9" class="extiw" title="de:Edgar Jaffé">de</a>)</span> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GRMW_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRMW-39">[39]</a></sup> His new interests would lie in more fundamental issues of social sciences; his works from this latter period are of primary interest to modern scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-166_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-166-35">[35]</a></sup> In 1904, Weber began to publish some of his most seminal papers in this journal, notably his essay <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i>, which became his most famous work<sup id="cite_ref-EESoc-22_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EESoc-22-40">[40]</a></sup> and laid the foundations for his later research on the impact of cultures and religions on the development of economic systems.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This essay was the only one of his works from that period that was published as a book during his lifetime. Some other of his works written in the first one and a half decades of the 20th century&#160;– published posthumously and dedicated primarily from the fields of sociology of religion, economic and legal sociology&#160;– are also recognised as among his most important intellectual contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also in 1904, he visited the United States and participated in the Congress of Arts and Sciences held in connection with the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Fair" class="mw-redirect" title="World's Fair">World's Fair</a> (<a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase_Exposition" title="Louisiana Purchase Exposition">Louisiana Purchase Exposition</a>) in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a>. A monument to his visit was placed at the home of relatives whom Weber visited in Mt. Airy, North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite his partial recovery evident in America, Weber felt that he was unable to resume regular teaching at that time and continued on as a private scholar, helped by an inheritance in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-Lachmann1970_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lachmann1970-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bendix3_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix3-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1909, disappointed with the <i>Verein</i>, he co-founded the <a href="/wiki/German_Sociological_Association" title="German Sociological Association">German Sociological Association</a> (<i>Deutsche Gesellschaft für Soziologie</i>, or DGS) and served as its first treasurer.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> He would, however, resign from the DGS in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1912, Weber tried to organise a left-wing political party to combine <a href="/wiki/Social-democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="Social-democrats">social-democrats</a> and liberals. This attempt was unsuccessful, in part because many liberals feared social-democratic revolutionary ideals.<sup id="cite_ref-TPaSToMW_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPaSToMW-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_involvements">Political involvements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Political involvements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Weber_1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Max_Weber_1917.jpg/220px-Max_Weber_1917.jpg" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Max_Weber_1917.jpg/330px-Max_Weber_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Max_Weber_1917.jpg/440px-Max_Weber_1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="657" data-file-height="385" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Weber_1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Max Weber (foreground) in 1917 with <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Toller" title="Ernst Toller">Ernst Toller</a> (facing)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Weber, aged 50, volunteered for service and was appointed as a reserve officer and put in charge of organizing the army hospitals in Heidelberg, a role he fulfilled until the end of 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix3_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix3-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kaesler_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaesler-44">[44]</a></sup> Weber's views on the war and the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/German_colonial_empire" title="German colonial empire">German empire</a> changed during the course of the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-TPaSToMW_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPaSToMW-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kaesler_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaesler-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Early on he supported the nationalist rhetoric and the war effort, though with some hesitation as he viewed the war as a necessity to fulfill German duty as a leading state power. In time, however, Weber became one of the most prominent critics of German expansionism and of the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Kaiser</a>'s war policies.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> He publicly attacked the <a href="/wiki/Schlieffen_Plan" title="Schlieffen Plan">Belgian annexation policy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unrestricted_submarine_warfare" title="Unrestricted submarine warfare">unrestricted submarine warfare</a> and later supported calls for constitutional reform, democratisation and <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber joined the <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers' council">worker and soldier council</a> of Heidelberg in 1918. He then served in the German delegation to the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> and as advisor to the Confidential Committee for Constitutional Reform, which drafted the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Constitution" title="Weimar Constitution">Weimar Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix3_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix3-38">[38]</a></sup> Motivated by his understanding of the American model, he advocated a strong, <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">popularly elected</a> presidency as a constitutional counterbalance to the power of the professional <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> More controversially, he also defended the provisions for emergency presidential powers that became <a href="/wiki/Article_48_(Weimar_Constitution)" title="Article 48 (Weimar Constitution)">Article 48</a> of the Weimar Constitution. These provisions were later used by <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> to subvert the rest of the constitution and institute rule by decree, allowing his regime to suppress opposition and gain dictatorial powers.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber also ran, unsuccessfully, for a parliamentary seat, as a member of the liberal <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Party" title="German Democratic Party">German Democratic Party</a>, which he had co-founded.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SchieVoermann2006_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SchieVoermann2006-47">[47]</a></sup> He opposed both the leftist <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%931919" class="mw-redirect" title="German Revolution of 1918–1919">German Revolution of 1918–1919</a> and the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, principled positions that defied the political alignments in Germany at that time,<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> and which may have prevented <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ebert" title="Friedrich Ebert">Friedrich Ebert</a>, the new social-democratic President of Germany, from appointing Weber as minister or ambassador.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaesler_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaesler-44">[44]</a></sup> Weber commanded widespread respect but relatively little influence.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Weber_and_German_politics" title="Weber and German politics">Weber's role in German politics</a> remains controversial to this day.</p>
<p>In the case of Weber's critique of the left, he complained that the leaders of the leftist <a href="/wiki/Spartacus_League" title="Spartacus League">Spartacus League</a> which was led by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Liebknecht" title="Karl Liebknecht">Karl Liebknecht</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosa_Luxemburg" title="Rosa Luxemburg">Rosa Luxemburg</a> and controlled the city government of Berlin while Weber was campaigning for his party: "We have this [German] revolution to thank for the fact that we cannot send a single division against the Poles. All we see is dirt, muck, dung, and horse-play—nothing else. Liebknecht belongs in the madhouse and Rosa Luxemburg in the zoological gardens.” <sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Weber was at the same time critical of the <a href="/wiki/Versailles_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Versailles Treaty">Versailles Treaty</a> which he believed unjustly assigned "war guilt" to Germany when it came to World War I. Weber believed that many countries were guilty of starting World War I, not just Germany. In making this case, Weber argued that “In the case of this war there is one, and only one power that desired it under all circumstances through its own will and, according to their political goals required: Russia. …It never crossed [my] mind that a German invasion of Belgium [in 1914] was nothing but an innocent act on the part of the Germans."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters201520_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters201520-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later that same month, in January 1919, after Weber and Weber's party were defeated for election, Weber delivered one of his greatest academic lectures, "<a href="/wiki/Politics_as_a_Vocation" title="Politics as a Vocation">Politics as a Vocation</a>", which was a reflection on the inherent violence and dishonesty found among the politicians, a profession in which only recently Weber was so personally active. About the nature of politicians, he concluded that "In nine out of ten cases they are windbags puffed up with hot air about themselves. They are not in touch with reality, and they do not feel the burden they need to shoulder; they just intoxicate themselves with romantic sensations."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters201521.2C_196_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters201521.2C_196-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_weber.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Max_weber.JPG/220px-Max_weber.JPG" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Max_weber.JPG/330px-Max_weber.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Max_weber.JPG/440px-Max_weber.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1496" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_weber.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Weber's grave in Heidelberg</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Frustrated with politics, Weber resumed teaching during this time, first at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>, then, after 1919, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Munich">University of Munich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lachmann1970_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lachmann1970-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bendix3_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix3-38">[38]</a></sup> His lectures from that period were collected into major works, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Wirtschaftsgeschichte" title="Wirtschaftsgeschichte">General Economic History</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Science_as_a_Vocation" title="Science as a Vocation">Science as a Vocation</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_as_a_Vocation" title="Politics as a Vocation">Politics as a Vocation</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> In Munich, he headed the first German university institute of sociology, but never held a professorial position in sociology. Many colleagues and students in Munich attacked his response to the German Revolution and some right-wing students held protests in front of his home.<sup id="cite_ref-TPaSToMW_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPaSToMW-43">[43]</a></sup> Max Weber contracted the <a href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">Spanish flu</a> and died of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> in Munich on 14 June 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> At the time of his death, Weber had not finished writing his <i>magnum opus</i> on sociological theory: <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i>. His widow Marianne helped prepare it for its publication in 1921–22.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Max_Weber.27s_thought">Max Weber's thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Max Weber's thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Max Weber's bureaucratic theory or model is sometimes also known as the "Legal-Rational" model. The model tries to explain bureaucracy from a rational point of view via nine main characteristics or principles; these are as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Ibrahim.2C_2012_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ibrahim.2C_2012-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Max_Weber.27s_Bureaucratic_Model_.28Legal-Rational_Model.29">Max Weber's Bureaucratic Model (Legal-Rational Model)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Max Weber's Bureaucratic Model (Legal-Rational Model)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weber wrote that the modern bureaucracy in both the public and private sector relies on the following principles.</p>
<p>"First, it is based on the general principle of precisely defined and organized across-the-board competencies of the various offices. These competencies are underpinned by rules, laws, or administrative regulations."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> For Weber, this means<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>A rigid division of labor is established which clearly identifies the regular tasks and duties of the particular bureaucratic system.</li>
<li>There are firmly established chains of command, and the duties and capacity to coerce others to comply is described by regulation.</li>
<li>Regular and continuous execution of the assigned duties is undertaken by hiring people with particular qualifications which are certified.</li>
</ol>
<p>Weber notes that these three aspects "constitute the essence of bureaucratic administration...in the public sector. In the private sector, these three aspects constitute the essence of a bureaucratic management of a private company."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Main Principles (Characteristics):</p>
<ol>
<li>Specialized roles.</li>
<li>Recruitment based on merit (e.g. tested through open competition).</li>
<li>Uniform principles of placement, promotion, and transfer in an administrative system.</li>
<li>Careerism with systematic salary structure.</li>
<li>Hierarchy, responsibility and accountability.</li>
<li>Subjection of official conduct to strict rules of discipline and control.</li>
<li>Supremacy of abstract rules.</li>
<li>Impersonal authority. (e.g. Office bearer does not bring the office with him).</li>
<li>Political neutrality.</li>
</ol>
<p>Merits: Max Weber himself noted, real bureaucracy will be less optimal and effective than his ideal type model. Each of Weber's principles can degenerate, more so, when it is utilized to analyze the individual level in the organization. But when implemented in a group setting in an organization, some form of efficiency and effectiveness can be achieved, especially with regards to better output. This is especially true when the Bureaucratic model emphasizes qualification (merits), specialization of job-scope (labour), hierarchy of power, rules and discipline.<sup id="cite_ref-James_W._Fesler.2C_1965_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_W._Fesler.2C_1965-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Demerits: However, competencies, efficiency and effectiveness can be unclear and contradictory especially when dealing with oversimplified matters. In a dehumanized bureaucracy, inflexible in distributing the job-scope, with every worker having to specialize from day one without rotating tasks for fear of decreasing output, tasks are often routine and can contribute to boredom. Thus, employees can sometimes feel that they are not part of the organization's work vision and missions. Consequently, they do not have any sense of belonging in the long term. Furthermore, this type of organization tends to invite exploitation and underestimate the potential of the employees, as creativity of the workers is brushed aside in favour of strict adherence to rules, regulations and procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-Ibrahim.2C_2012_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ibrahim.2C_2012-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspirations">Inspirations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Inspirations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weber's thinking was strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/German_idealism" title="German idealism">German idealism</a> and particularly by <a href="/wiki/Neo-Kantianism" title="Neo-Kantianism">neo-Kantianism</a>, to which he had been exposed through <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rickert" title="Heinrich Rickert">Heinrich Rickert</a>, his professorial colleague at the University of Freiburg.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Especially important to Weber's work is the neo-Kantian belief that reality is essentially chaotic and incomprehensible, with all rational order deriving from the way in which the human mind focuses its attention on certain aspects of reality and organises the resulting perceptions.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber's opinions regarding the methodology of the social sciences show parallels with the work of contemporary neo-Kantian philosopher and pioneering sociologist <a href="/wiki/Georg_Simmel" title="Georg Simmel">Georg Simmel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber was also influenced by <a href="/wiki/Kantian_ethics" title="Kantian ethics">Kantian ethics</a>, which he nonetheless came to think of as obsolete in a modern age lacking in religious certainties. In this last respect, the influence of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche's philosophy</a> is evident.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> According to the <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i>, the "deep tension between the Kantian moral imperatives and a Nietzschean diagnosis of the modern cultural world is apparently what gives such a darkly tragic and agnostic shade to Weber's ethical worldview".<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Another major influence in Weber's life was the writings of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and the workings of socialist thought in academia and active politics. While Weber shares some of Marx's consternation with bureaucratic systems and maligns them as being capable of advancing their own logic to the detriment of human freedom and autonomy, Weber views conflict as perpetual and inevitable and does not host the spirit of a materially available utopia.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Though the influence of his mother's Calvinist religiosity is evident throughout Weber's life and work, and though he maintained a deep, lifelong interest in the study of religions, Weber was open about the fact that he was personally irreligious.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a political economist and economic historian, Weber belonged to the "youngest" German <a href="/wiki/Historical_school_of_economics" title="Historical school of economics">historical school of economics</a>, represented by academics such as <a href="/wiki/Gustav_von_Schmoller" title="Gustav von Schmoller">Gustav von Schmoller</a> and his student <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>. But, even though Weber's research interests were very much in line with that school, his views on methodology and the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_value_(economics)" title="Theory of value (economics)">theory of value</a> diverged significantly from those of other German historicists and were closer, in fact, to those of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Menger" title="Carl Menger">Carl Menger</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian School</a>, the traditional rivals of the historical school.<sup id="cite_ref-Swedberg-econ-soc_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swedberg-econ-soc-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup> (See section on <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber#Economics" title="Max Weber">Economics</a>.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodology">Methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg/220px-Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg" width="220" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg/330px-Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg/440px-Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="3168" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manuskript_max_weber_rechtssoziologie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A page from the typescript of the sociology of law within <a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike some other classical figures (Comte, Durkheim) Weber did not attempt, consciously, to create any specific set of rules governing social sciences in general, or sociology in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> In comparison with Durkheim and Marx, Weber was more focused on individuals and culture and this is clear in his methodology.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup> Whereas Durkheim focused on the society, Weber concentrated on the individuals and their actions (see <a href="/wiki/Structure_and_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Structure and action">structure and action</a> discussion) and whereas Marx argued for the primacy of the material world over the world of ideas, Weber valued ideas as motivating actions of individuals, at least in the big picture.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-165_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-165-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-144_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-144-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-148_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-148-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sociology, for Max Weber, is:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... a science which attempts the interpretive understanding of social action in order thereby to arrive at a causal explanation of its course and effects.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-Denhardt2010_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denhardt2010-64">[64]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber was concerned with the question of <a href="/wiki/Objectivity_(philosophy)" title="Objectivity (philosophy)">objectivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber distinguished <a href="/wiki/Social_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Social action">social action</a> from <a href="/wiki/Social_behavior" title="Social behavior">social behavior</a>, noting that social action must be understood through how individuals subjectively relate to one another.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-31_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-31-65">[65]</a></sup> Study of social action through <a href="/wiki/Verstehen" title="Verstehen">interpretive means</a> (<i>Verstehen</i>) must be based upon understanding the subjective meaning and purpose that individuals attach to their actions.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-166_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-166-35">[35]</a></sup> Social actions may have easily identifiable and objective means, but much more subjective ends and the understanding of those ends by a scientist is subject to yet another layer of subjective understanding (that of the scientist).<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber noted that the importance of subjectivity in social sciences makes creation of fool-proof, universal laws much more difficult than in <a href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural sciences">natural sciences</a> and that the amount of objective knowledge that social sciences may achieve is precariously limited.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Overall, Weber supported the goal of objective science, but he noted that it is an unreachable goal&#160;– although one definitely worth striving for.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is no absolutely "objective" scientific analysis of culture.... All knowledge of cultural reality ... is always knowledge from particular points of view.... an "objective" analysis of cultural events, which proceeds according to the thesis that the ideal of science is the reduction of empirical reality to "laws", is meaningless ... [because] ... the knowledge of social laws is not knowledge of social reality but is rather one of the various aids used by our minds for attaining this end.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber, <i>"Objectivity" in Social Science</i>, 1904<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The principle of "<a href="/wiki/Methodological_individualism" title="Methodological individualism">methodological individualism</a>", which holds that social scientists should seek to understand collectivities (such as nations, cultures, governments, churches, corporations, etc.) solely as the result and the context of the actions of individual persons, can be traced to Weber, particularly to the first chapter of <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i>, in which he argues that only individuals "can be treated as agents in a course of subjectively understandable action".<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-31_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-31-65">[65]</a></sup> In other words, Weber argued that social phenomena can be understood scientifically only to the extent that they are captured by models of the behaviour of purposeful individuals, models that Weber called "<a href="/wiki/Ideal_type" title="Ideal type">ideal types</a>", from which actual historical events will necessarily deviate due to accidental and irrational factors.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup> The analytical constructs of an ideal type never exist in reality, but provide objective benchmarks against which real-life constructs can be measured.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-149_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-149-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We know of no <a href="/wiki/Scientific_law" title="Scientific law">scientifically ascertainable ideals</a>. To be sure, that makes our efforts more arduous than in the past, since we are expected to create our ideals from within our breast in the very age of <a href="/wiki/Subjectivism" title="Subjectivism">subjectivist</a> culture.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber, 1909<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber's methodology was developed in the context of a wider debate about methodology of social sciences, the <i><a href="/wiki/Methodenstreit" title="Methodenstreit">Methodenstreit</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-166_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-166-35">[35]</a></sup> Weber's position was close to <a href="/wiki/Historicism" title="Historicism">historicism</a>, as he understood social actions as being heavily tied to particular historical contexts and its analysis required the understanding of subjective motivations of individuals (social actors).<sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-166_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-166-35">[35]</a></sup> Thus Weber's methodology emphasises the use of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_history" title="Comparative history">comparative historical analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-153_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-153-69">[69]</a></sup> Therefore, Weber was more interested in explaining how a certain outcome was the result of various historical processes rather than predicting an outcome of those processes in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-148_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-148-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationalisation">Rationalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rationalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many scholars have described <a href="/wiki/Rationalization_(sociology)" title="Rationalization (sociology)">rationalisation</a> and the question of individual freedom in an increasingly rational society, as the main theme of Weber's work.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-167_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-167-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-30_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-30-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-151_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-151-72">[72]</a></sup> This theme was situated in the larger context of the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Psychological" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychological">psychological</a> motivations, cultural values and beliefs (primarily, religion) and the structure of the society (usually determined by the economy).<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-148_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-148-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>By rationalisation, Weber understood first, the individual cost-benefit calculation, second, the wider, bureaucratic organisation of the organisations and finally, in the more general sense as the opposite of understanding the reality through mystery and magic (<a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">disenchantment</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-151_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-151-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The fate of our times is characterised by rationalisation and intellectualisation and, above all, by the "disenchantment of the world"</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-Koshul2005_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koshul2005-73">[73]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber began his studies of the subject in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i>, in which he argued that the redefinition of the connection between work and piety in Protestantism and especially in <a href="/wiki/Ascetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">denominations</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, shifted human effort towards rational efforts aimed at achieving economic gain.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix60_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix60-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-162_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-162-75">[75]</a></sup> In Protestant religion, Christian piety towards God was expressed through one's secular vocation (secularisation of <a href="/wiki/Calling_(religious)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calling (religious)">calling</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-162_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-162-75">[75]</a></sup> The rational roots of this doctrine, he argued, soon grew incompatible with and larger than the religious and so the latter were eventually discarded.<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_J._Weigert_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_J._Weigert-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber continued his investigation into this matter in later works, notably in his studies on <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a> and on the classification of legitimate <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> into three types&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Rational-legal_authority" title="Rational-legal authority">Rational-legal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_authority" title="Traditional authority">traditional</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_authority" title="Charismatic authority">charismatic</a>&#160;– of which the rational-legal (through bureaucracy) is the dominant one in the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> In these works Weber described what he saw as society's movement towards rationalisation.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Similarly, rationalisation could be seen in the economy, with the development of highly rational and calculating capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber also saw rationalisation as one of the main factors setting the European West apart from the rest of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Rationalisation relied on deep changes in ethics, religion, psychology and culture; changes that first took place in the Western civilisation.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What Weber depicted was not only the secularisation of Western <i>culture</i>, but also and especially the development of modern <i>societies</i> from the viewpoint of rationalisation. The new structures of society were marked by the differentiation of the two functionally intermeshing systems that had taken shape around the organisational cores of the capitalist enterprise and the bureaucratic state apparatus. Weber understood this process as the institutionalisation of purposive-rational economic and administrative action. To the degree that everyday life was affected by this cultural and societal rationalisation, traditional forms of life&#160;– which in the early modern period were differentiated primarily according to one's trade&#160;– were dissolved.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a>, <i>Modernity's Consciousness of Time</i>, 1990 [1985]<sup id="cite_ref-Habermas.2C_J.C3.BCrgen_1990_p2_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Habermas.2C_J.C3.BCrgen_1990_p2-11">[11]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Features of rationalisation include increasing knowledge, growing impersonality and enhanced control of social and material life.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Weber was ambivalent towards rationalisation; while admitting it was responsible for many advances, in particular, freeing humans from traditional, restrictive and illogical social guidelines, he also criticised it for dehumanising individuals as "cogs in the machine" and curtailing their freedom, trapping them in the bureaucratic <a href="/wiki/Iron_cage" title="Iron cage">iron cage</a> of rationality and bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-167_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-167-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-38-42_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-38-42-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-177_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-177-78">[78]</a></sup> Related to rationalisation is the process of <a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">disenchantment</a>, in which the world is becoming more explained and less mystical, moving from <a href="/wiki/Polytheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a> religions to <a href="/wiki/Monotheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> ones and finally to the Godless science of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Those processes affect all of society, removing "<a href="/wiki/Sublime_(philosophy)" title="Sublime (philosophy)">sublime</a> values... from public life" and making art less creative.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-151-152_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-151-152-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Dystopian" class="mw-redirect" title="Dystopian">dystopian</a> critique of rationalisation, Weber notes that modern society is a product of an <a href="/wiki/Individualistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Individualistic">individualistic</a> drive of the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>, yet at the same time, the society created in this process is less and less welcoming of individualism.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>How is it at all possible to salvage any remnants of "individual" freedom of movement in any sense given this all-powerful trend?</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociology_of_religion">Sociology of religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sociology of religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weber's work in the field of <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociology of religion</a> started with the essay <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i> and continued with the analysis of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_China:_Confucianism_and_Taoism" title="The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism">The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_India:_The_Sociology_of_Hinduism_and_Buddhism" title="The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism">The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Judaism_(book)" title="Ancient Judaism (book)">Ancient Judaism</a></i>. His work on other religions was interrupted by his sudden death in 1920, which prevented him from following <i>Ancient Judaism</i> with studies of early Christianity and Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix285_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix285-80">[80]</a></sup> His three main themes in the essays were the effect of religious ideas on economic activities, the relation between <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a> and religious ideas and the distinguishable characteristics of Western civilisation.<sup id="cite_ref-BendixChapter9_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BendixChapter9-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber saw religion as one of the core forces in society.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-153_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-153-69">[69]</a></sup> His goal was to find reasons for the different development paths of the cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Occident</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Orient" title="Orient">Orient</a>, although without judging or valuing them, like some of the contemporary thinkers who followed the <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinist</a> paradigm; Weber wanted primarily to explain the distinctive elements of the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western civilisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BendixChapter9_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BendixChapter9-81">[81]</a></sup> In the analysis of his findings, Weber maintained that <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> (and more widely, Protestant) religious ideas had had a major impact on the <a href="/wiki/Social_innovation" title="Social innovation">social innovation</a> and development of the economic system of the West, but noted that they were not the only factors in this development. Other notable factors mentioned by Weber included the <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> of scientific pursuit, merging observation with mathematics, science of scholarship and jurisprudence, rational systematisation and bureaucratisation of government administration and economic enterprise.<sup id="cite_ref-BendixChapter9_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BendixChapter9-81">[81]</a></sup> In the end, the study of the sociology of religion, according to Weber, focused on one distinguishing part of the Western culture, the decline of beliefs in <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a>, or what he referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">disenchantment</a> of the world".<sup id="cite_ref-BendixChapter9_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BendixChapter9-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber also proposed a <a href="/wiki/Socioevolutionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Socioevolutionary">socioevolutionary</a> model of religious change, showing that in general, societies have moved from magic to <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> and finally, <a href="/wiki/Ethical_monotheism" title="Ethical monotheism">ethical monotheism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-154_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-154-82">[82]</a></sup> According to Weber, this evolution occurred as the growing economic stability allowed <a href="/wiki/Professionalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Professionalisation">professionalisation</a> and the evolution of ever more sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Priesthood" class="mw-redirect" title="Priesthood">priesthood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-155_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-155-83">[83]</a></sup> As societies grew more complex and encompassed different groups, a hierarchy of gods developed and as power in the society became more centralised, the concept of a single, universal God became more popular and desirable.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-158_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-158-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism"><i>The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/170px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/255px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/340px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="1124" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cover of a German edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Weber's essay <i>The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</i> is his most famous work.<sup id="cite_ref-EESoc-22_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EESoc-22-40">[40]</a></sup> It is argued<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> that this work should not be viewed as a detailed study of Protestantism, but rather as an introduction into Weber's later works, especially his studies of interaction between various religious ideas and economic behaviour as part of the rationalisation of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economic system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> In <i>The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</i>, Weber put forward the thesis that <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist ethic</a> and ideas influenced the development of capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> He noted the post-Reformation shift of Europe's economic centre away from Catholic countries such as France, Spain and Italy, and toward Protestant countries such as the Netherlands, England, Scotland and Germany. Weber also noted that societies having more Protestants were those with a more highly developed capitalist economy.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Similarly, in societies with different religions, most successful business leaders were Protestant.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> Weber thus argued that Roman Catholicism impeded the development of the capitalist economy in the West, as did other religions such as <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> elsewhere in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
