<p>The development of the concept of the calling quickly gave to the modern entrepreneur a fabulously clear conscience&#160;– and also industrious workers; he gave to his employees as the wages of their ascetic devotion to the calling and of co-operation in his ruthless exploitation of them through capitalism the prospect of eternal salvation.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-162_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-162-75">[75]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Christian religious devotion had historically been accompanied by rejection of mundane affairs, including economic pursuit.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix57_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix57-87">[87]</a></sup> Weber showed that certain types of Protestantism&#160;– notably <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>&#160;– were supportive of rational pursuit of economic gain and worldly activities dedicated to it, seeing them as endowed with moral and spiritual significance.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix60_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix60-74">[74]</a></sup> Weber argued that there were many reasons to look for the origins of modern capitalism in the religious ideas of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix54_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix54-88">[88]</a></sup> In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_ethic" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant ethic">Protestant ethic</a> (or more specifically, Calvinist ethic) motivated the believers to work hard, be successful in business and reinvest their profits in further development rather than frivolous pleasures.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> The notion of <a href="/wiki/Calling_(religious)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calling (religious)">calling</a> meant that each individual had to take action as an indication of their salvation; just being a member of the Church was not enough.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-162_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-162-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">Predestination</a> also reduced agonising over <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a> and further, it meant that a material wealth could be taken as a sign of <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> in the afterlife.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup> The believers thus justified pursuit of profit with religion, as instead of being fuelled by morally suspect greed or ambition, their actions were motivated by a highly moral and respected philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> This Weber called the "spirit of capitalism": it was the Protestant religious ideology that was behind&#160;– and inevitably led to&#160;– the capitalist economic system.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-35-37_85-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-35-37-85">[85]</a></sup> This theory is often viewed as a reversal of Marx's thesis that the <a href="/wiki/Base_and_superstructure" title="Base and superstructure">economic "base"</a> of society determines all other aspects of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix60_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix60-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber abandoned research into Protestantism because his colleague <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Troeltsch" title="Ernst Troeltsch">Ernst Troeltsch</a>, a professional theologian, had begun work on the book <i>The Social Teachings of the Christian Churches and Sects</i>. Another reason for Weber's decision was that Troeltsch's work already achieved what he desired in that area: laying the groundwork for a comparative analysis of religion and society.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix49_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix49-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The phrase "<a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">work ethic</a>" used in modern commentary is a derivative of the "<a href="/wiki/Protestant_ethic" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant ethic">Protestant ethic</a>" discussed by Weber. It was adopted when the idea of the Protestant ethic was generalised to apply to the Japanese people, Jews and other non-Christians and thus lost its religious connotations.<sup id="cite_ref-SwedbergAgevall2005_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SwedbergAgevall2005-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Religion_of_China:_Confucianism_and_Taoism"><i>The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_China:_Confucianism_and_Taoism" title="The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism">The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism</a></div>
<p><i>The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism</i> was Weber's second major work on the sociology of religion. Hans H. Gerth edited and translated this text into English, with an introduction by C. K. Wang.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Weber focused on those aspects of Chinese society that were different from those of Western Europe, especially those aspects that contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Puritanism#Beliefs" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a>. His work also questioned why capitalism did not develop in China.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix98_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix98-93">[93]</a></sup> He focused on the issues of Chinese urban development, Chinese <a href="/wiki/Patrimonialism" title="Patrimonialism">patrimonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Officialdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Officialdom">officialdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">Chinese religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophy in China">philosophy</a> (primarily, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>), as the areas in which Chinese development differed most distinctively from the European route.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix98_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix98-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Weber, Confucianism and Puritanism are mutually exclusive types of <a href="/wiki/Rationalization_(sociology)" title="Rationalization (sociology)">rational thought</a>, each attempting to prescribe a way of life based on religious dogma.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix135_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix135-94">[94]</a></sup> Notably, they both valued self-control and restraint and did not oppose accumulation of wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix135_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix135-94">[94]</a></sup> However, to both those qualities were just means to the final goal and here they were divided by a key difference.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup> Confucianism's goal was "a cultured status position", while Puritanism's goal was to create individuals who are "tools of God".<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix135_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix135-94">[94]</a></sup> The intensity of belief and enthusiasm for action were rare in Confucianism, but common in Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix135_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix135-94">[94]</a></sup> Actively working for wealth was unbecoming a proper Confucian.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup> Therefore, Weber states that it was this difference in social attitudes and mentality, shaped by the respective, dominant religions, that contributed to the development of capitalism in the West and the absence of it in China.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix135_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix135-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Religion_of_India:_The_Sociology_of_Hinduism_and_Buddhism"><i>The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_India:_The_Sociology_of_Hinduism_and_Buddhism" title="The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism">The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism</a></div>
<p><i>The Religion of India: The Sociology of Hinduism and Buddhism</i> was Weber's third major work on the sociology of religion. In this work he deals with the structure of Indian society, with the <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Heterodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterodox">heterodox</a> doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, with modifications brought by the influence of popular religiosity and finally with the impact of religious beliefs on the secular ethic of Indian society.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix142_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix142-95">[95]</a></sup> In Weber's view, Hinduism in India, like Confucianism in China, was a barrier for capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">Indian caste system</a> made it very difficult for individuals to advance in the society beyond their caste.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup> Activity, including economic activity, was seen as unimportant in the context of the advancement of the <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-37-38_89-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-37-38-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber ended his research of society and religion in India by bringing in insights from <a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_China:_Confucianism_and_Taoism" title="The Religion of China: Confucianism and Taoism">his previous work on China</a> to discuss similarities of the Asian belief systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup> He notes that the beliefs saw the meaning of life as otherworldly <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystical</a> experience.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup> The social world is fundamentally divided between the educated elite, following the guidance of a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> or wise man and the uneducated masses whose beliefs are centered on magic.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup> In Asia, there was no <a href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Messianic prophecy">Messianic prophecy</a> to give <i>plan and meaning to the everyday life of educated and uneducated alike</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup> Weber juxtaposed such Messianic prophecies (also called <a href="/wiki/Ethical_prophecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethical prophecy">ethical prophecies</a>), notably from the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> region to the <a href="/wiki/Exemplary_prophecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Exemplary prophecy">exemplary prophecies</a> found on the Asiatic mainland, focused more on reaching to the educated elites and enlightening them on the proper ways to live one's life, usually with little emphasis on hard work and the material world.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bendix90_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix90-97">[97]</a></sup> It was those differences that prevented the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Occident</a> from following the paths of the earlier Chinese and Indian civilisations. His next work, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Judaism_(book)" title="Ancient Judaism (book)">Ancient Judaism</a> was an attempt to prove this theory.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix199_96-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix199-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Judaism"><i>Ancient Judaism</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ancient Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Judaism_(book)" title="Ancient Judaism (book)">Ancient Judaism (book)</a></div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Judaism_(book)" title="Ancient Judaism (book)">Ancient Judaism</a></i>, his fourth major work on the sociology of religion, Weber attempted to explain the factors that resulted in the early differences between <a href="/wiki/Oriental" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental">Oriental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Occidental</a> <a href="/wiki/Religiosity" title="Religiosity">religiosity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix200_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix200-98">[98]</a></sup> He contrasted the innerworldly <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a> developed by Western Christianity with mystical contemplation of the kind developed in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix200_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix200-98">[98]</a></sup> Weber noted that some aspects of Christianity sought to conquer and change the world, rather than withdraw from its imperfections.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix200_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix200-98">[98]</a></sup> This fundamental characteristic of Christianity (when compared to Far Eastern religions) stems originally from ancient Jewish <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix204_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix204-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber claimed that Judaism not only fathered Christianity and Islam, but was crucial to the rise of the modern Occidental state; Judaism's influence was as important as Hellenistic and Roman cultures.</p>
<p>Weber's premature death in 1920 prevented him from following his planned analysis of <a href="/wiki/Psalm" class="mw-redirect" title="Psalm">Psalms</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">Book of Job</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talmudic" class="mw-redirect" title="Talmudic">Talmudic</a> Jewry, early Christianity and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_Society"><i>Economy and Society</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economy and Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></div>
<p>Weber's magnum opus <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i> is a collection of his essays which he was working on at the time of his death in 1920. After his death, the final organization and editing of the book fell to his widow <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Weber" title="Marianne Weber">Marianne Weber</a>. The final German form published in 1921 reflected very much Marianne Weber's work and intellectual commitment. Beginning in 1956, the German jurist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Johannes_Wincklemann&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Johannes Wincklemann (page does not exist)">Johannes Wincklemann</a> began editing and organizing the German edition of Economy and Society based on his study of the papers that Weber left at his death.</p>
<p>English versions of Economy and Society were published as a collected volume in 1968 as edited by Gunther Roth and Claus Wittich. As a result of the various editions in German and English, there are differences between the organization of the different volumes.</p>
<p>Economy and Society includes a wide range of essays dealing with Weber's views regarding Sociology, Social Philosophy, Politics, Social Stratification, World Religion, Diplomacy, and other subjects. The book is typically published in a two volume set in both German and English, and is more than 1000 pages long.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theodicy_of_Fortune_and_Misfortune">Theodicy of Fortune and Misfortune</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Theodicy of Fortune and Misfortune">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Theodicy of fortune and misfortune within sociology is the theory, as Weber suggested, of how "members of different social classes adopt different belief systems, or theodices, to explain their social situation".<sup id="cite_ref-Plye.2C_Davidson_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plye.2C_Davidson-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The concept of theodicy was expanded mainly with the thought of Weber and his addition of ethical considerations to the subject of religion. There is this ethical part of religion, including "(1) soteriology and (2) theodicy. These mean, respectively, how people understand themselves to be able to be in a <i>correct</i> relationship with supernatural powers, and how to explain evil – or why bad things seem to happen to those who seem to be good people".<sup id="cite_ref-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_7_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_7-101">[101]</a></sup> There is a separation of different theodicies with regard to class. "Theodicies of misfortune tend to the belief that wealth and other manifestations of privilege are indications or signs of evil.... In contrast, theodicies of fortune emphasise the notion that privileges are a blessing and are deserved."<sup id="cite_ref-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_7_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_7-101">[101]</a></sup> Weber also writes that "the affluent embrace good fortune theodicies, which emphasise that prosperity is a blessing of God...[while] theodices of misfortune emphasise that affluence is a sign of evil and that suffering in this world will be rewarded in the next."<sup id="cite_ref-Plye.2C_Davidson_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plye.2C_Davidson-100">[100]</a></sup> Thus these two distinctions can be applied not only to class structure within society but denomination and racial segregation within religion.</p>
<p>Weber defines the importance of societal class within religion by examining the difference between the two theodicies and to what class structures they apply. The concept of "work ethic" is attached to the theodicy of fortune; thus, because of the Protestant "work ethic", there was a contribution of higher class outcomes and more education among Protestants.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Those without the work ethic clung to the theodicy of misfortune, believing wealth and happiness were granted in the afterlife. Another example of how this belief of religious theodicy influences class, is that those of lower status, the poor, cling to deep religiousness and faith as a way to comfort themselves and provide hope for a more prosperous future, while those of higher status cling to the sacraments or actions that prove their right of possessing greater wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-Plye.2C_Davidson_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plye.2C_Davidson-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>These two theodicies can be found in the denominational segregation within the religious community. The main division can be seen between the mainline Protestant and evangelical denominations and their relation to the class into which their particular theodicy pertains. For example, mainline churches, with their upper class congregations, "promote[d] order, stability, and conservatism, and in so doing proved to be a powerful source of legitimation of the status quo and of existing disparities in the distribution of wealth and power" because much of the wealth of the church comes from the congregation.<sup id="cite_ref-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_137_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_137-103">[103]</a></sup> In contrast, Pentecostal churches adopted the theodicy of misfortune. They instead "advocated change intended to advance the cause of justice and fairness".<sup id="cite_ref-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_137_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christiano.2C_Swatos.2C_Kivisto_2008_137-103">[103]</a></sup> Thus the learned and upper class religious churches who preach the theodicy of fortune, ultimately support capitalism and corporation, while the churches who adopted the theodicy of misfortune, instead preached equality and fairness.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_government">Politics and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Politics and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Political_sociology" title="Political sociology">political sociology</a>, one of Weber's most influential contributions is his "<a href="/wiki/Politics_as_a_Vocation" title="Politics as a Vocation">Politics as a Vocation</a>" (<i>Politik als Beruf</i>) essay. Therein, Weber unveils the definition of the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> as that entity that possesses a <a href="/wiki/Monopoly_on_the_legitimate_use_of_physical_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Monopoly on the legitimate use of physical force">monopoly on the legitimate use of physical force</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-E-PoV_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E-PoV-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Weber wrote that politics is the sharing of state's power between various groups, and political leaders are those who wield this power.<sup id="cite_ref-E-PoV_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E-PoV-105">[105]</a></sup> A politician must not be a man of the "true Christian ethic", understood by Weber as being the ethic of the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, that is to say, the injunction to turn the other cheek.<sup id="cite_ref-PaV_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaV-107">[107]</a></sup> An adherent of such an ethic ought rather to be understood to be a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a>, for it is only saints, according to Weber, that can appropriately follow it.<sup id="cite_ref-PaV_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaV-107">[107]</a></sup> The political realm is no realm for saints; a politician ought to marry the <i>ethic of attitude</i> and the <i>ethic of responsibility</i> ("<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Verantwortungsethik" class="extiw" title="de:Verantwortungsethik">Verantwortungsethik</a> vs <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gesinnungsethik" class="extiw" title="de:Gesinnungsethik">Gesinnungsethik</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>) and must possess both a passion for his vocation and the capacity to distance himself from the subject of his exertions (the governed).<sup id="cite_ref-PaV_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaV-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber distinguished three <a href="/wiki/Ideal_type" title="Ideal type">ideal types</a> of political leadership (alternatively referred to as three types of domination, legitimisation or authority):<sup id="cite_ref-Ibrahim.2C_2012_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ibrahim.2C_2012-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charismatic_domination" class="mw-redirect" title="Charismatic domination">charismatic domination</a> (<a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">familial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Religion and politics">religious</a>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Traditional_domination" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional domination">traditional domination</a> (<a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarchs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patrimonialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>) and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Legal_domination" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal domination">legal domination</a> (modern law and state, <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-WolfJM_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WolfJM-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>In his view, every historical relation between rulers and ruled contained such elements and they can be analysed on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_classification_of_authority" title="Tripartite classification of authority">this tripartite distinction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix296_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix296-111">[111]</a></sup> He notes that the instability of charismatic authority forces it to "routinise" into a more structured form of authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-38-42_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-38-42-77">[77]</a></sup> In a pure type of traditional rule, sufficient resistance to a ruler can lead to a "traditional revolution". The move towards a <a href="/wiki/Rational-legal" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational-legal">rational-legal</a> structure of authority, utilising a bureaucratic structure, is inevitable in the end.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix303_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix303-112">[112]</a></sup> Thus this theory can be sometimes viewed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Social_evolutionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Social evolutionism">social evolutionism</a> theory. This ties to his broader concept of <a href="/wiki/Rationalization_(sociology)" title="Rationalization (sociology)">rationalisation</a> by suggesting the inevitability of a move in this direction.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-38-42_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-38-42-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Bureaucratic administration means fundamentally domination through knowledge.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber described many ideal types of public administration and government in his masterpiece <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i> (1922). His critical study of the bureaucratisation of society became one of the most enduring parts of his work.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-38-42_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-38-42-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup> It was Weber who began the studies of bureaucracy and whose works led to the popularisation of this term.<sup id="cite_ref-BuSach_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BuSach-114">[114]</a></sup> Many aspects of modern <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> go back to him and a classic, hierarchically organised civil service of the Continental type is called "Weberian civil service".<sup id="cite_ref-Hooghe2001_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooghe2001-115">[115]</a></sup> As the most efficient and rational way of organising, bureaucratisation for Weber was the key part of the rational-legal authority and furthermore, he saw it as the key process in the ongoing rationalisation of the Western society.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-38-42_77-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-38-42-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber listed several preconditions for the emergence of the bureaucracy:<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-172-176_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-172-176-116">[116]</a></sup> The growth in space and population being administered, the growth in complexity of the administrative tasks being carried out and the existence of a <a href="/wiki/Monetary_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Monetary economy">monetary economy</a>&#160;– these resulted in a need for a more efficient administrative system.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-172-176_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-172-176-116">[116]</a></sup> Development of <a href="/wiki/Information_and_communications_technology" title="Information and communications technology">communication</a> and transportation technologies made more efficient administration possible (and popularly requested) and <a href="/wiki/Democratisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratisation">democratisation</a> and rationalisation of culture resulted in demands that the new system treat everybody equally.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-172-176_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-172-176-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber's ideal bureaucracy is characterised by hierarchical organisation, by delineated lines of authority in a fixed area of activity, by action taken (and recorded) on the basis of written rules, by bureaucratic officials needing expert training, by rules being implemented neutrally and by career advancement depending on technical qualifications judged by organisations, not by individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-172-176_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-172-176-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The decisive reason for the advance of the bureaucratic organisation has always been its purely technical superiority over any other form of organisation.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-Hooghe2001_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooghe2001-115">[115]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>While recognising bureaucracy as the most efficient form of organisation and even indispensable for the modern state, Weber also saw it as a threat to individual freedoms and the ongoing bureaucratisation as leading to a "polar night of icy darkness", in which increasing rationalisation of human life traps individuals in the aforementioned "<a href="/wiki/Iron_cage" title="Iron cage">iron cage</a>" of bureaucratic, rule-based, rational control.<sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GRitzer_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRitzer-117">[117]</a></sup> In order to counteract bureaucrats, the system needs entrepreneurs and politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-Bureaucratic_113-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureaucratic-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_stratification">Social stratification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Social stratification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Weber also formulated a <a href="/wiki/Three-component_theory_of_stratification" title="Three-component theory of stratification">three-component theory of stratification</a>, with social class, <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">social status</a> and political party as conceptually distinct elements.<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix85_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix85-118">[118]</a></sup> The three-component theory of stratification is in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> simpler theory of social class which ties all social stratification to what people own. In Weber's theory, issues of honour and prestige are important. This distinction is most clearly described in Weber's essay "Classes, Staende, Parties" which was first published in his book Economy and Society.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The three components of Weber's theory are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Social Class which is based on economically determined relationship to the market (owner, <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">renter</a>, employee etc.).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">Status</a> (or in German <i>Stand</i>) which is based on non-economic qualities like honour, prestige and religion.</li>
<li>Party which refers to affiliations in the political domain.</li>
</ul>
<p>All three dimensions have consequences for what Weber called "<a href="/wiki/Life_chances" title="Life chances">life chances</a>" (opportunities to improve one's life).<sup id="cite_ref-Bendix85_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix85-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber scholars recognize that it is important to maintain a sharp distinction between the terms "status" and "class," even though they tend to be used interchangeably in popular usage.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Study_of_the_city">Study of the city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Study of the city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of his overarching effort to understand the unique development of the Western world, Weber produced a detailed general study of the city as the characteristic locus of the social and economic relations, political arrangements, and ideas that eventually came to define the West. This resulted in a monograph titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_City_(book)" title="The City (book)">The City</a></i>, which was probably compiled from research conducted in 1911–<a href="/wiki/1913" title="1913">13</a>, and which was published posthumously in 1921. In 1924 it was incorporated into the second part of his <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i>, as chapter XVI, "The City (Non-legitimate Domination)".</p>
<p>According to Weber, the city as a politically autonomous organisation of people living in close proximity, employed in a variety of specialised trades, and physically separated from the surrounding countryside, only fully developed in the West and to a great extent shaped its cultural evolution:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The origin of a rational and inner-worldly ethic is associated in the Occident with the appearance of thinkers and prophets [...] who developed in a social context that was alien to the Asiatic cultures. This context consisted of the political problems engendered by the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeois</a> status-group of the city, without which neither Judaism, nor Christianity, nor the development of Hellenistic thinking are conceivable.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-City_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-City-121">[121]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber argued that <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, early Christianity, theology, and later the political party and modern science, were only possible in the urban context that reached a full development in the West alone.<sup id="cite_ref-SwedbergAgevall-city_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SwedbergAgevall-city-122">[122]</a></sup> He also saw in the history of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> European cities the rise of a unique form of "non-legitimate domination" that successfully challenged the existing forms of legitimate domination (traditional, charismatic, and rational-legal) that had prevailed until then in the Medieval world.<sup id="cite_ref-SwedbergAgevall-nonlegitimate_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SwedbergAgevall-nonlegitimate-123">[123]</a></sup> This new domination was based on the great economic and military power wielded by the organised community of city-dwellers ("citizens").</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weber regarded himself primarily as a "<a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economist</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-ScienceVoc_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceVoc-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WPet_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPet-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PRBaehr_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PRBaehr-126">[126]</a></sup> and all of his professorial appointments were in economics, though today his contributions in that field are largely overshadowed by his role as a founder of modern sociology. As an economist, Weber belonged to the "youngest" German <a href="/wiki/Historical_school_of_economics" title="Historical school of economics">historical school of economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-capa_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capa-127">[127]</a></sup> The great differences between that school's interests and methods on the one hand and those of the <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_economics" title="Neoclassical economics">neoclassical school</a> (from which modern <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_economics" title="Mainstream economics">mainstream economics</a> largely derives) on the other, explain why Weber's influence on economics today is hard to discern.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Max Weber was a pioneer in delineating a connection between capitalism and exceptionalism. In earlier texts, <a href="/wiki/Maximiliano_Korstanje" title="Maximiliano Korstanje">Maximiliano Korstanje</a> explores the American exceptionalism and its effects on the current means of productions. This contribution to the existent literature is of paramount importance because of two main reasons. The first and most important, he relates capitalism to Protestantism. This is of course nothing new, since Max Weber did it in the past. However, Korstanje adds, though Weber was in the correct side by confirming the connection of Reform and Capitalism, the key role played by Norse Mythology was left behind. In effect, the concept of predestination as it has been discussed by Weber did not come from Luther alone. It was rather enrooted in the core of Norse Culture. Secondly, this impossibility to understand the future led to English speaking societies to construct a risk-prone culture, which resulted in what <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_Beck" title="Ulrich Beck">Ulrich Beck</a> dubbed the “Risk Society”. The criticism exerted in the uneven distribution of Capitalism should be explained by the “economy of salved peoples” formulated by Weber.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodological_individualism">Methodological individualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Methodological individualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Though his research interests were always in line with those of the German historicists, with a strong emphasis on interpreting <a href="/wiki/Economic_history" title="Economic history">economic history</a>, Weber's defence of "<a href="/wiki/Methodological_individualism" title="Methodological individualism">methodological individualism</a>" in the social sciences represented an important break with that school and an embracing of many of the arguments that had been made against the historicists by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Menger" title="Carl Menger">Carl Menger</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian School</a> of economics, in the context of the academic <i><a href="/wiki/Methodenstreit" title="Methodenstreit">Methodenstreit</a></i> ("debate over methods") of the late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup> The phrase "methodological individualism", which has come into common usage in modern debates about the connection between <a href="/wiki/Microeconomics" title="Microeconomics">microeconomics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomics" title="Macroeconomics">macroeconomics</a>, was coined by the Austrian-American economist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a> in 1908 as a way of referring to the views of Weber.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup> According to Weber's theses, social research cannot be fully <a href="/wiki/Induction_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Induction (philosophy)">inductive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">descriptive</a>, because <i>understanding</i> some phenomenon implies that the researcher must go beyond mere description and <i>interpret</i> it; interpretation requires classification according to abstract "<a href="/wiki/Ideal_type" title="Ideal type">ideal (pure) types</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-capa_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capa-127">[127]</a></sup> This, together with his antipositivistic argumentation (see <i><a href="/wiki/Verstehen" title="Verstehen">Verstehen</a></i>), can be taken as a methodological justification for the model of the "<a href="/wiki/Rational_economic_man" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational economic man">rational economic man</a>" (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_economicus" title="Homo economicus">homo economicus</a></i>), which is at the heart of modern <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_economics" title="Mainstream economics">mainstream economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordIndividualism_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordIndividualism-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-capa_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capa-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Marginalism_and_psychophysics">Marginalism and psychophysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Marginalism and psychophysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Unlike other historicists, Weber also accepted the <a href="/wiki/Marginalism" title="Marginalism">marginal theory of value</a> (also called "marginalism") and taught it to his students.<sup id="cite_ref-Swedberg-econ-soc_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swedberg-econ-soc-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schweitzer_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer-133">[133]</a></sup> In 1908, Weber published an article in which he drew a sharp methodological distinction between psychology and economics and attacked the claims that the marginal theory of value in economics reflected the form of the psychological response to stimuli as described by the <a href="/wiki/Weber-Fechner_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Weber-Fechner law">Weber-Fechner law</a>. Max Weber's article has been cited as a definitive refutation of the dependence of the economic theory of value on the laws of <a href="/wiki/Psychophysics" title="Psychophysics">psychophysics</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Robbins" title="Lionel Robbins">Lionel Robbins</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Stigler" title="George Stigler">George Stigler</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>, though the broader issue of the relation between economics and psychology has come back into the academic debate with the development of "<a href="/wiki/Behavioral_economics" title="Behavioral economics">behavioral economics</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_history">Economic history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economic history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Weber's best known work in economics concerned the preconditions for capitalist development, particularly the relations between religion and capitalism, which he explored in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i> as well as in his other works on the <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociology of religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-capa_127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capa-127">[127]</a></sup> He argued that bureaucratic political and economic systems emerging in the Middle Ages were essential in the rise of modern capitalism (including rational book-keeping and organisation of formally free labour), while they were a hindrance in the case of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient world">ancient</a> capitalism, which had a different social and political structure based on conquest, slavery, and the coastal city-state.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Other contributions include his early work on the <a href="/wiki/Die_R%C3%B6mische_Agrargeschichte_in_ihrer_Bedeutung_f%C3%BCr_das_Staats-_und_Privatrecht" class="mw-redirect" title="Die Römische Agrargeschichte in ihrer Bedeutung für das Staats- und Privatrecht">economic history of Roman agrarian society</a> (1891) and on the <a href="/wiki/Condition_of_Farm_Labour_in_Eastern_Germany" title="Condition of Farm Labour in Eastern Germany">labour relations in Eastern Germany</a> (1892), his analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Zur_Geschichte_der_Handelsgesellschaften_im_Mittelalter" title="Zur Geschichte der Handelsgesellschaften im Mittelalter">history of commercial partnerships in the Middle Ages</a> (1889), his critique of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, the discussion of the roles of <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> in the history of capitalism in his <i><a href="/wiki/Economy_and_Society" title="Economy and Society">Economy and Society</a></i> (1922) and his <i><a href="/wiki/Wirtschaftsgeschichte" title="Wirtschaftsgeschichte">General Economic History</a></i> (1923), a notable example of the kind of empirical work associated with the German Historical School.<sup id="cite_ref-capa_127-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capa-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although today Weber is primarily read by sociologists and <a href="/wiki/Social_philosophy" title="Social philosophy">social philosophers</a>, Weber's work did have a significant influence on <a href="/wiki/Frank_Knight" title="Frank Knight">Frank Knight</a>, one of the founders of the neoclassical <a href="/wiki/Chicago_school_of_economics" title="Chicago school of economics">Chicago school of economics</a>, who translated Weber's <i>General Economic History</i> into English in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-Emmett_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emmett-137">[137]</a></sup> Knight also wrote in 1956 that Max Weber was the only economist who dealt with the problem of understanding the emergence of modern capitalism "from the angle which alone can yield an answer to such questions, that is, the angle of comparative history in the broad sense".<sup id="cite_ref-Schweitzer_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_calculation">Economic calculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economic calculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Weber, like his colleague <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>, regarded <a href="/wiki/Cost-benefit_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Cost-benefit analysis">economic calculation</a> and especially the <a href="/wiki/Double-entry_bookkeeping_system" title="Double-entry bookkeeping system">double-entry bookkeeping</a> method of business accounting, as one of the most important forms of <a href="/wiki/Rationalization_(sociology)" title="Rationalization (sociology)">rationalisation</a> associated with the development of modern capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-SwedbergAgevall2005-22_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SwedbergAgevall2005-22-138">[138]</a></sup> Weber's preoccupation with the importance of economic calculation led him to develop a critique of socialism as a system that lacked a mechanism for allocating resources efficiently in order to satisfy human needs.<sup id="cite_ref-Tribe_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribe-139">[139]</a></sup> Socialist intellectuals like <a href="/wiki/Otto_Neurath" title="Otto Neurath">Otto Neurath</a> had realised that in a completely socialised economy, <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">prices</a> would not exist and central planners would have to resort to in-kind (rather than monetary) <a href="/wiki/Economic_calculation_problem" title="Economic calculation problem">economic calculation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tribe_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribe-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StanfordNeurathSup_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordNeurathSup-140">[140]</a></sup> According to Weber, this type of coordination would be inefficient, especially because it would be incapable of solving the problem of <a href="/wiki/Imputation_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Imputation (economics)">imputation</a> (i.e. of accurately determining the relative values of <a href="/wiki/Capital_good" title="Capital good">capital goods</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Tribe_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribe-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StanfordNeurathSup_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordNeurathSup-140">[140]</a></sup> Weber wrote that, under full socialism,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In order to make possible a rational utilisation of the means of production, a system of in-kind accounting would have to determine "value"&#160;– indicators of some kind for the individual capital goods which could take over the role of the "prices" used in book valuation in modern business accounting. But it is not at all clear how such indicators could be established and in particular, verified; whether, for instance, they should vary from one production unit to the next (on the basis of economic location), or whether they should be uniform for the entire economy, on the basis of "social utility", that is, of (present and future) consumption requirements [...] Nothing is gained by assuming that, if only the problem of a non-monetary economy were seriously enough attacked, a suitable accounting method would be discovered or invented. The problem is fundamental to any kind of complete socialisation. We cannot speak of a rational "planned economy" so long as in this decisive respect we have no instrument for elaborating a rational "plan".</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber<sup id="cite_ref-EconSoc-calculation_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconSoc-calculation-141">[141]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>This argument against socialism was made independently, at about the same time, by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tribe_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tribe-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hulsmann_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hulsmann-142">[142]</a></sup> Weber himself had a significant influence on Mises, whom he had befriended when they were both at the University of Vienna in the spring of 1918,<sup id="cite_ref-Mises-Weber_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mises-Weber-143">[143]</a></sup> and, through Mises, on several other economists associated with the Austrian School in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Haberler_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haberler-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> in particular elaborated the arguments of Weber and Mises about economic calculation into a central part of free market economics's intellectual assault on socialism, as well as into a model for the spontaneous coordination of "<a href="/wiki/Dispersed_knowledge" title="Dispersed knowledge">dispersed knowledge</a>" in markets.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HayekNobel_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HayekNobel-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The prestige of Max Weber among European social scientists would be difficult to over-estimate. He is widely considered the greatest of German sociologists and ... has become a leading influence in European and American thought.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_Heinrich_Gerth&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hans Heinrich Gerth (page does not exist)">Hans Heinrich Gerth</a> and <a href="/wiki/C._Wright_Mills" title="C. Wright Mills">C. Wright Mills</a>, <i>From Max Weber: Essays in Sociology</i>, 1946/1991<sup id="cite_ref-WeberGerth1991_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WeberGerth1991-6">[6]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber's most influential work was on <a href="/wiki/Economic_sociology" title="Economic sociology">economic sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_sociology" title="Political sociology">political sociology</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociology of religion</a>. Along with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WPet_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPet-125">[125]</a></sup> he is commonly regarded as one of the founders of modern sociology. But whereas Durkheim, following <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Comte</a>, worked in the <a href="/wiki/Sociological_positivism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sociological positivism">positivist</a> tradition, Weber was instrumental in developing an <a href="/wiki/Antipositivism" title="Antipositivism">antipositivist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermeneutic">hermeneutic</a>, tradition in the <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JKRhoads_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JKRhoads-148">[148]</a></sup> In this regard he belongs to a similar tradition as his German colleagues <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Simmel" title="Georg Simmel">Georg Simmel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Dilthey" title="Wilhelm Dilthey">Wilhelm Dilthey</a>, who stressed the differences between the methodologies appropriate to the social and the natural sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-JKRhoads_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JKRhoads-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber presented sociology as the science of human <a href="/wiki/Social_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Social action">social action</a>; action that he separated into <a href="/wiki/Traditional_action" title="Traditional action">traditional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Affectional_action" title="Affectional action">affectional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Value-rational_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Value-rational action">value-rational</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Instrumental action">instrumental</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferrante_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrante-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritzer2009-33_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritzer2009-33-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[Sociology is] the science whose object is to interpret <i>the meaning of social action</i> and thereby give a <i>causal explanation</i> of the way in which the <i>action proceeds</i> and the <i>effects which it produces</i>. By "action" in this definition is meant the human behaviour when and to the extent that the agent or agents see it as <i>subjectively meaningful</i> [...] the meaning to which we refer may be either (a) the meaning actually intended either by an individual agent on a particular historical occasion or by a number of agents on an approximate average in a given set of cases, or (b) the meaning attributed to the agent or agents, as types, in a pure type constructed in the abstract. In neither case is the "meaning" to be thought of as somehow objectively "correct" or "true" by some metaphysical criterion. This is the difference between the empirical sciences of action, such as sociology and history and any kind of <i>a priori</i> discipline, such as jurisprudence, logic, ethics, or aesthetics whose aim is to extract from their subject-matter "correct" or "valid" meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Max Weber, <i>The Nature of Social Action</i>, 1922</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In his own time, however, Weber was viewed primarily as a historian and an economist.<sup id="cite_ref-WPet_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPet-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PRBaehr_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PRBaehr-126">[126]</a></sup> The breadth of Weber's topical interests is apparent in the depth of his <a href="/wiki/Social_theory" title="Social theory">social theory</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The affinity between capitalism and Protestantism, the religious origins of the Western world, the force of charisma in religion as well as in politics, the all-embracing process of rationalisation and the bureaucratic price of progress, the role of legitimacy and of violence as the offspring of leadership, the "disenchantment" of the modern world together with the never-ending power of religion, the antagonistic relation between intellectualism and eroticism: all these are key concepts which attest to the enduring fascination of Weber's thinking.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Radkau" title="Joachim Radkau">Joachim Radkau</a>, <i>Max Weber: A Biography</i>, 2005<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Many of Weber's works famous today were collected, revised and published <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_work" class="mw-redirect" title="Posthumous work">posthumously</a>. Significant interpretations of his writings were produced by such sociological luminaries as <a href="/wiki/Talcott_Parsons" title="Talcott Parsons">Talcott Parsons</a> and C. Wright Mills. Parsons in particular imparted to Weber's works a functionalist, <a href="/wiki/Teleological" class="mw-redirect" title="Teleological">teleological</a> perspective; this personal interpretation has been criticised for a latent conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weber has influenced many later social theorists, such as <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Adorno" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodor Adorno">Theodor Adorno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Horkheimer" title="Max Horkheimer">Max Horkheimer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gy%C3%B6rgy_Luk%C3%A1cs" title="György Lukács">György Lukács</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> Different elements of his thought were emphasised by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schmitt" title="Carl Schmitt">Carl Schmitt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Lachmann" title="Ludwig Lachmann">Ludwig Lachmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Aron" title="Raymond Aron">Raymond Aron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_5-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-5">[5]</a></sup> According to Austrian economist <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a>, who had met Weber during his time at the University of Vienna,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The early death of this genius was a great disaster for Germany. Had Weber lived longer, the German people of today would be able to look to this example of an "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan</a>" who would not be broken by <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">National Socialism</a>.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a>, 1940<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Weber's friend, the psychiatrist and existentialist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Karl_Jaspers" title="Karl Jaspers">Karl Jaspers</a>, described him as "the greatest German of our era". Weber's untimely death felt to Jaspers "as if the German world had lost its heart".<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_responses_to_Weber">Critical responses to Weber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Critical responses to Weber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Weber's explanations are highly specific to the historical periods he analysed.<sup id="cite_ref-AllanAllan2005-150-151_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllanAllan2005-150-151-156">[156]</a></sup> Some academics disagree, pointing out that, despite the fact that Weber did write in the early twentieth century, his ideas remain alive and relevant for understanding issues like politics, bureaucracy, and social stratification today.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters20151.E2.80.9318_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatersWaters20151.E2.80.9318-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many scholars, however, have disagreed with specific claims Weber makes in his historical analysis. For example, the economist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a> argued that capitalism did not begin with the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> but in 14th century Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, Venice and <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, the small <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">city-state</a> governments led to the development of the earliest forms of capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> was a commercial centre of Europe. Also, the predominantly Calvinist country of Scotland did not enjoy the same economic growth as the Netherlands, England and New England. It has been pointed out that the Netherlands, which had a Calvinist majority, industrialised much later in the 19th century than predominantly Catholic Belgium, which was one of the centres of the Industrial Revolution on the European mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Emil Kauder expanded Schumpeter's argument by arguing the hypothesis that <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> hurt the development of capitalism by leading to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_theory_of_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour theory of value">labour theory of value</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For an extensive list of Max Weber's works, see <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber_bibliography" title="Max Weber bibliography">Max Weber bibliography</a>.</p>
<p>Weber wrote in German. Original titles printed after his death (1920) are most likely <a href="/wiki/Anthology" title="Anthology">compilations</a> of his unfinished works (of the <i>Collected Essays…</i> form). Many translations are made of parts or sections of various German <a href="/wiki/Original" class="mw-redirect" title="Original">originals</a> and the names of the translations often do not reveal what part of German work they contain. Weber's work is generally quoted according to the critical <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.mohr.de/en/multi-volume-work/max-weber-gesamtausgabe-323700000"><i>Max Weber-Gesamtausgabe</i></a> (Collected Works edition), which is published by Mohr Siebeck in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCbingen" title="Tübingen">Tübingen</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Weber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
